Compact Textbooks in Mathematics 
This textbook series presents concise introductions to current topics in mathematics 
and mainly addresses advanced undergraduates and master students. The concept 
is to offer small books covering subject matter equivalent to 2- or 3-hour lectures 
or seminars which are also suitable for self-study. The books provide students and 
teachers with new perspectives and novel approaches. They may feature examples 
and exercises to illustrate key concepts and applications of the theoretical contents. 
The series also includes textbooks speciï¬cally speaking to the needs of students 
from other disciplines such as physics, computer science, engineering, life sciences, 
ï¬nance.

compact: small books presenting the relevant knowledge 
â€¢ 
learning made easy: examples and exercises illustrate the application of the 
contents 
â€¢ 
useful for lecturers: each title can serve as basis and guideline for a semester 
course/lecture/seminar of 2-3 hours per week.

1
Introduction and Preliminaries 
In this ï¬rst chapter we give an introduction and outline of the topics from the book. 
We also introduce basic notions and results from linear algebra, convexity theory 
and real algebraic geometry. 
1.1
Introduction 
Optimization, an important branch of mathematics, is becoming ever more inï¬‚uen-
tial with steadily increasing computational power. Convex optimization is consid-
ered to be its most accessible form, allowing for efï¬cient numerical and geometrical 
approaches, such as interior point and subgradient methods, as well as duality 
theory. Many of the solution methods are of polynomial complexity, at least up 
to arbitrary precision, see [5,13] for a comprehensive general overview. 
For a long time, linear programming has been, and still is, the most important 
subclass of convex programming, and a thorough theory of linear programming 
has been established, combined with highly effective solution procedures (see for 
example [19,20]). The feasible sets of linear programming are polytopes/polyhedra, 
convex sets deï¬ned by ï¬nitely many afï¬ne linear inequalities. The study of 
polyhedra from a geometric point of view reaches far back in history, but it 
also remains a challenging branch of modern research, see for example [106]. 
Polyhedra are of an intrinsically combinatorial nature, which allows to apply special 
geometric and algebraic methods to study them. On the other hand, this clearly 
hinders applicability of linear programming to problems which are not of such 
combinatorial ï¬‚avor. 
Semideï¬nite programming, a much more recent development, can come to the 
rescue here (see Sect. B.3 from the Appendix for an exact deï¬nition). As a still 
restrictive class of convex optimization problems, it allows for efï¬cient numerical 
solution schemes, mostly based on interior point methods with barrier function (see 
for example [66,101,102,105]). Furthermore, several restrictions imposed by linear 
Â© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2023 
T. Netzer, D. Plaumann, Geometry of Linear Matrix Inequalities, Compact Textbooks 
in Mathematics, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-26455-9_1

1
Introduction and Preliminaries
programming disappear. One of the ï¬rst signiï¬cant applications stems from [36], 
where the stable set problem for graphs was translated into a semideï¬nite program, 
solvable in polynomial time for perfect graphs. In recent times, semideï¬nite 
programming has found numerous other applications [3,12,21,56,76]. 
The feasible sets of semideï¬nite programming are called spectrahedra. They are  
sets deï¬ned by linear matrix inequalities, i.e., by the condition that a symmetric 
matrix with afï¬ne linear entries is positive semideï¬nite. This involves the spectrum 
of matrices and generalizes polyhedra, which explains the name [86]. Spectrahedra 
are very special convex sets. They share some important properties with polyhedra, 
but also generalize these to a great extend. Furthermore, linear images of spectrahe-
dra, so-called spectrahedral shadows, are equally useful for optimization, and they 
extend the class of convex sets even more. 
The study of spectrahedra and their shadows as geometric objects is much 
younger, among the earliest works seem to be [59, 77, 86] from the 1990s. Again, 
the area has seen tremendous progress in recent years, combining results and 
methods from such diverse areas as (real) algebraic geometry, convexity theory, 
operator algebra, discrete geometry and optimization. Among the landmark results 
are surely Ramana and Goldman [86] with a ï¬rst systematic study of the geometry of 
spectrahedra, the relaxation techniques by Lasserre [56,57] and Parrilo [76], Helton 
and Vinnikovâ€™s [43] classiï¬cation of spectrahedra in dimension two (which also 
gives a solution to the Lax conjecture [60,61]), the positive results of Helton and Nie 
on spectrahedral shadows [41, 42], and Scheidererâ€™s classiï¬cation of spectrahedral 
shadows in dimension two [98], as well as his obstructions in higher dimensions 
[99]. All these insights are not only fascinating from a geometric point of view, but 
can also help to develop more efï¬cient optimization algorithms and extend their 
ï¬eld of application. 
Of particular relevance in optimization are questions as the following: Given a 
certain set (often described by polynomial inequalities), what is a good description 
of its convex hull? Can semideï¬nite programming be performed on this convex hull, 
i.e., is it a spectrahedron or a spectrahedral shadow? If so, ï¬nd explicit descriptions 
by linear matrix inequalities. If not, ï¬nd good inner or outer approximations by sets 
on which semideï¬nite programming can be performed. Try to make the optimization 
as efï¬cient as possible, by ï¬nding semideï¬nite presentations of smallest possible 
size and complexity. 
The goal of this book is to give an up-to-date presentation of the most important 
results on the geometry of spectrahedra and their projections. Even though the 
motivation comes from computational problems and in particular from optimization, 
our focus will mostly be on geometry and theoretical foundations. We tried to keep 
the exposition as elementary as possible, to make the book useful for pure and 
applied mathematicians, and hopefully even for engineers with a solid background 
in mathematics. Many of the results should be accessible with good a knowledge of 
linear algebra, some require more background from algebra and (real) algebraic 
geometry. We provide some of this non-standard background material in the 
appendix. We point out that [9] partially covers similar subjects, combining chapters

1.2
Preliminaries
3
by different authors. That book, however, is much more focused on applications and 
less on the geometric aspects that we explain here. 
This book is organized as follows. Chapter 2 is devoted to a systematic study of 
spectrahedra. We give deï¬nitions and explain ï¬rst important properties in Sects. 2.1 
and 2.2. These sections also contain large classes of examples. In Sects. 2.3 and 2.4 
we explain the connections between spectrahedra, hyperbolic polynomials and 
determinantal representations. This culminates in a proof of the Helton-Vinnikov 
Theorem on two-dimensional spectrahedra in Sect. 2.5. Section 2.6 explains con-
nections between graphs and spectrahedra, proving BrÃ¤ndÃ©nâ€™s results on elementary 
symmetric polynomials and derivative cones. Section 2.8 contains an overview of 
the theory of non-commutative spectrahedra and their relations to operator systems. 
Chapter 3 treats linear images of spectrahedra, so-called spectrahedral shadows. 
After introducing the concept in Sect. 3.1, we review in Sect. 3.2 the many construc-
tions they permit. The important Lasserre-Parrilo relaxation method is explained 
in Sect. 3.3. Based on this, we prove Scheidererâ€™s result on spectrahedral shadows 
in the plane in Sect. 3.4. Section 3.5 contains the results of Helton and Nie on 
exactness of relaxations, and they are applied to hyperbolicity cones in Sect. 3.6. 
After presenting some general obstructions to the relaxation method in Sect. 3.7, the  
main part of the book closes with Sect. 3.8 and Scheidererâ€™s examples of convex 
semialgebraic sets that are not spectrahedral shadows. 
The Appendix contains two parts. In Sect. A we collect necessary background 
material on real algebraic geometry, most notably Archimedean PositivstellensÃ¤tze 
for polynomials and matrix polynomials. Section B explains some concepts and 
results from convexity theory and optimization. 
This book presents many wonderful results of our colleagues. Among them 
are Petter BrÃ¤ndÃ©n, JoÃ£o Gouveia, Bill Helton, Igor Klep, Mario Kummer, Jean 
Lasserre, Pablo Parrilo, Mihai Putinar, Claus Scheiderer, Konrad SchmÃ¼dgen, 
Markus Schweighofer, Rainer Sinn, Andreas Thom, Victor Vinnikov and Cynthia 
Vinzant. We thank them all for numerous discussions on the topic of this book. 
A preliminary version was used as lecture notes for courses at the Universities of 
Innsbruck, Konstanz and Leipzig. We thank our students for the interest, and for 
many remarks and questions that helped improving the exposition a lot. All graphics 
in this book were created with Mathematica [104].

2
Linear Matrix Inequalities and Spectrahedra 
In this chapter we introduce the notion of a spectrahedron, and thoroughly study its 
properties. We will see many examples, and learn methods to determine whether a 
given set is a spectrahedron or not. In most cases we will also obtain procedures to 
explicitly construct deï¬ning linear matrix inequalities. 
2.1
Spectrahedra 
A polyhedron is a subset of double struck upper R Superscript nRn described by a ï¬nite number of afï¬ne linear 
inequalities. Compact polyhedra are exactly the polytopes, i.e., the convex hulls 
of ï¬nitely many points. These are the simplest (and the most extensively studied) 
convex sets. Spectrahedra, our basic objects, are generalizations of polyhedra that 
comprise many more sets, but still share some of the good properties of polyhedra. 
Just as polyhedra are the feasible sets of linear progamming, spectrahedra are the 
feasible sets of semideï¬nite programming. 
Deï¬nition 2.1 
A spectrahedron in double struck upper R Superscript nRn is the inverse image of upper S y m Subscript s Superscript plusSym+
s under an afï¬ne linear map 
double struck upper R Superscript n Baseline right arrow upper S y m Subscript sRn â†’Syms, for  some s element of double struck upper Ns âˆˆN. 
Many of the basic geometric properties we explain in this section can already 
be found in [86]. Spectrahedra are clearly convex, since the inverse image of any 
convex set under an afï¬ne linear map is again convex (Fig. 2.1). Occasionally, we 
may ï¬nd it convenient not to ï¬x coordinates and consider a spectrahedron in a ï¬nite-
dimensional real vector space V given as the inverse image of upper S y m left parenthesis upper W right parenthesis Superscript plusSym(W)+ under 
an afï¬ne linear map upper Phi colon upper V right arrow upper S y m left parenthesis upper W right parenthesis: V â†’Sym(W), for some ï¬nite-dimensional real vector 
space W. Here, upper S y m left parenthesis upper W right parenthesisSym(W) denotes the space of symmetric bilinear forms on W, and 
upper S y m left parenthesis upper W right parenthesis Superscript plusSym(W)+ denotes the convex cone of positive semideï¬nite forms.

of ordinary linear inequalities: If upper M left parenthesis x right parenthesisM(x) is a linear matrix polynomial, then

One can make a theory of spectrahedra deï¬ned by Hermitian matrices, which 
may be the natural point of view for certain questions (for example in quantum 
physics). However, in terms of the class of sets that one obtains, this does not add 
anything new, essentially due to the following simple observation. We will thus 
mostly restrict to real symmetric matrix polynomials in the following.

linear matrix polynomial N of size 2 s times 2 s2s Ã— 2s such that

Deï¬nition 2.11 
A linear matrix polynomial M of size s is called monic if upper M left parenthesis 0 right parenthesis equals upper I Subscript sM(0) = Is, the identity 
matrix.

â–¶Remark 2.15 Lemma 2.12 essentially says that it is enough to consider spectra-
hedra deï¬ned by monic linear matrix polynomials. For if S is any spectrahedron, 
let V be its afï¬ne hull. Then S has nonempty relative interior in V , and after a 
translation we may assume that 0 is in the relative interior of S. We may then 
change coordinates, replace double struck upper R Superscript nRn by V , and assume that S is given by a monic 
linear matrix polynomial. 
While this argument works ï¬ne as a ï¬rst reduction step in a proof, actually 
computing a monic representation in large examples can be a difï¬cult and 
computationally expensive task, which is usually avoided whenever possible.

First Properties of Spectrahedra

2.2
First Properties of Spectrahedra 
In general it can be very hard to decide whether a given set is a spectrahedron or not. 
It might even be harder to ï¬nd an explicit linear matrix polynomial that describes 
the set. We will thus start with some easy properties that all spectrahedra share, i.e., 
we will state necessary conditions for a set to be a spectrahedron. 
Proposition 2.20 
Spectrahedra are convex and basic closed semialgebraic. 
Proof Let upper S equals script upper S left parenthesis upper M right parenthesisS = S(M) for some linear matrix polynomial M of size s. Convexity 
of S is clear from the deï¬nition. We must show that there exist polynomials

semideï¬nite for u element of double struck upper R Superscript nu âˆˆRn is saying that all its eigenvalues are nonnegative. The 
eigenvalues are the roots of the characteristic polynomial

of

the cone of univariate nonnegative polynomials. Show that C is closed, convex and

So this set is not a spectrahedron, and thus clearly the cone of globally nonnegative 
univariate polynomials of degree at most 4 is not a spectrahedron. Note that all 
nonnegative polynomials in one variable are sums of squares of polynomials (see 
for example [63,83]). 
Another property of spectrahedra is that all their faces are exposed, see Sect. B.2 
for the deï¬nition and some properties. This was ï¬rst proven in [86].

Finally, there is a very restrictive necessary condition for being a spectrahedron, 
called real zero property (or hyperbolicity, in the homogeneous setting). This 
property comes from the fact that symmetric matrices have real eigenvalues, and 
it in fact entails all the already mentioned properties of spectrahedra. If upper M equals upper I Subscript s Baseline plus sigma summation Underscript i equals 1 Overscript n Endscripts x Subscript i Baseline upper M Subscript iM =

2.3
Hyperbolic Polynomials 
Hyperbolicity is the homogeneous version of the real-zero property introduced 
above. We will now deï¬ne and study it in detail. It is closely connected to 
spectrahedra, but also to partial differential equations [30, 47, 75] and discrete 
structures [15,16,38], and allows for direct convex optimization procedures, called 
hyperbolic programming [4,37,89].

Let h element of double struck upper R left bracket x 0 comma ellipsis comma x Subscript n Baseline right bracketh âˆˆR[x0, . . . , xn] be hyperbolic with respect to e. The  set

is called the (closed) hyperbolicity cone of h with respect to e. 
In this sense, one can think of hyperbolic polynomials as generalized charac-
teristic polynomials, and the hyperbolicity cones as generalized cones of positive 
semideï¬nite matrices. But in spite of the name, it is not apparent from the deï¬nition 
that ModifyingAbove script upper C With quotation dash Subscript e Baseline left parenthesis h right parenthesisCe(h) is indeed a convex cone. Also, the name suggests that h should be 
hyperbolic with respect to any point in the (interior of the) hyperbolicity cone. These 
statements can be proved directly, as is done for example in [33]. Instead, we will 
deduce them later from the Helton-Vinnikov Theorem in Sect. 2.5. See Fig. 2.6 for 
an illustration of a hyperbolicity cone. 
In Example 2.35 (5), we have already seen that the hyperbolicity condition is 
necessary for a cone to be spectrahedral.

let h element of double struck upper R left bracket x 0 comma ellipsis comma x Subscript n Baseline right bracket minus StartSet 0 EndSeth âˆˆR[x0, . . . , xn]\{0} be the unique (up to scaling) polynomial of minimal

âŠ“âŠ”
â–¶Remark 2.39 Theorem 2.38 is useful for checking whether a cone is spectra-
hedral. The polynomial h is often given with the deï¬nition of C, or can easily 
be computed from it. Now hyperbolicity of h is a necessary condition for being 
spectrahedral. 
For example, it shows that the cone 
upper C equals left brace u element of double struck upper R cubed vertical bar u 0 Superscript 4 Baseline greater than or slanted equals u 1 Superscript 4 Baseline plus u 2 Superscript 4 Baseline comma u 0 greater than or slanted equals 0 right braceC = {u âˆˆR3 | u4

this also shows that the TV screen is not a spectrahedron. 
Whether hyperbolicity is also sufï¬cient for being spectrahedral is an open 
question, the so-called generalized Lax conjecture, which we will discuss below. 
Note that checking hyperbolicity of a polynomial will also be hard in general, as 
hard as checking polynomial nonnegativity [93]. At least, it is clear what needs 
to be checked, whereas the deï¬nition of a spectrahedron requires to produce the 
deï¬ning matrix polynomial, of which not much is known a priori, not even its 
size. There are also certain approaches to use sums-of-squares techniques for 
certifying hyperbolicity [22,44,69,85]. 
Deï¬nition 2.40 
Given a homogeneous polynomial h element of double struck upper R left bracket x 0 comma ellipsis comma x Subscript n Baseline right bracketh âˆˆR[x0, . . . , xn], and a homogeneous 
symmetric linear matrix polynomial M such that h equals det left parenthesis upper M right parenthesish = det(M), we say that M is a 
symmetric (linear) determinantal representation of h. If, in addition, upper M left parenthesis e right parenthesis greater than 0M(e) > 0
for some e element of double struck upper R Superscript n plus 1e âˆˆRn+1, then h is hyperbolic with respect to e, and we say that the 
determinantal representation is deï¬nite.

Now if we do the count of parameters in the above proof for n equals 2n = 2, we ï¬nd 
that the resulting inequality holds for all d. In 1957, it was conjectured by Peter 
Lax, in connection with the study of hyperbolic PDEs in [60], that every hyperbolic 
polynomial in three variables possesses a deï¬nite determinantal representation. This 
became known as the Lax conjecture. It was proved in [43] through the work of 
Vinnikov and Helton-Vinnikov.

2.3
Hyperbolic Polynomials
31
Theorem 2.44 (Helton-Vinnikov) 
Every hyperbolic polynomial in three variables x 0 comma x 1 comma x 2x0, x1, x2 possesses a deï¬nite 
symmetric determinantal representation. 
Corollary 2.45 
Every three-dimensional hyperbolicity cone is spectrahedral. 
We will not give a full proof of the Helton-Vinnikov Theorem. However, we 
will prove a weaker version in Sect. 2.5 below, namely the existence of deï¬nite 
Hermitian determinantal representations. This will still imply Corollary 2.45, using  
Lemma 2.9. 
Since it is clear from Proposition 2.42 that the Helton-Vinnikov Theorem cannot 
extend to the case n greater than or slanted equals 3n â©¾3, the search began for a suitable higher dimensional 
analogue. Various weaker versions have been proposed in recent years, some of 
which have been disproved (see for example [14,53,72]). Perhaps the most natural 
generalization is simply the statement of Corollary 2.45. 
Generalized Lax Conjecture: Every hyperbolicity cone is a spectrahedron. 
A few special cases of the conjecture are known, but in general it remains elusive. 
Example 2.46 (Quadratic Polynomials) For any hyperbolic polynomial of degree 2, the 
associated hyperbolicity cone is spectrahedral, independent of the dimension. Note that, 
in view of Proposition 2.42, this does not mean they all admit a deï¬nite determinantal 
representation. It can however be shown that some high enough power always admits a 
determinantal representation [72]. Since

2.4
Deï¬nite Determinantal Representations and Interlacing 
To prove the Helton-Vinnikov Theorem, it is helpful to understand it as a statement 
in two parts. The ï¬rst part amounts to the construction of determinantal repre-
sentations over double struck upper RR or double struck upper CC, the second to a characterization of those determinantal 
representations that are deï¬nite, and therefore reï¬‚ect the hyperbolicity. The follow-
ing notion will be used to address the second part.

2.5
Hyperbolic Curves and the Helton-Vinnikov Theorem 
The goal of this section is to show that every hyperbolic polynomial in three 
variables (also called a hyperbolic plane curve) possesses a Hermitian determinantal 
representation. This is a weaker statement than the Helton-Vinnikov theorem 
(Theorem 2.44), which says that there even exists a symmetric determinantal 
representation, but it still sufï¬ces to characterize three-dimensional spectahedral 
cones and plane spectrahedra. Our proof follows [81]. An alternative proof for the 
Hermitian case, using a different approach, can be found in [34]. 
First of all, we now again speak of curves and convex subsets of the plane, rather 
than of three-dimensional cones. This is because we consider projective varieties 
instead of afï¬ne cones, which gives a better geometric picture. We will use three 
variables x comma y comma zx, y, z in this section. A homogeneous polynomial h element of double struck upper R left bracket x comma y comma z right bracketh âˆˆR[x, y, z] of 
degree d deï¬nes the projective plane curve

Corollary 2.64 
Every hyperbolic polynomial in three variables possesses a deï¬nite Hermitian 
determinantal representation. 
Proof Suppose h element of double struck upper R left bracket x comma y comma z right bracketh âˆˆR[x, y, z] is irreducible and strictly hyperbolic with respect to 
e. Then the directional derivative partial differential Subscript e Baseline hâˆ‚eh strictly interlaces h and can be used an input 
in Construction 2.61. By Theorem 2.62 (2), this will result in a deï¬nite Hermitian 
determinantal representation of h. If  h is strictly hyperbolic but not irreducible, then 
each irreducible factor of h is strictly hyperbolic and we can build a Hermitian 
determinantal representation of h as a block matrix from the representations of all 
factors. 
In general, if h element of double struck upper R left bracket x comma y comma z right bracket Subscript left parenthesis d right parenthesish âˆˆR[x, y, z](d) is (not necessarily strictly) hyperbolic with respect 
to e and h left parenthesis e right parenthesis equals 1h(e) = 1, by Exercise  2.50 (2) there exists a sequence of strictly hyperbolic 
polynomials 
h Subscript k Baseline element of double struck upper R left bracket x comma y comma z right bracket Subscript left parenthesis d right parenthesishk âˆˆR[x, y, z](d)
(with respect to e) and h Subscript k Baseline left parenthesis e right parenthesis equals 1 commahk(e) = 1, converging to h. Now each h Subscript khk has a Hermitian 
determinantal representation, hence so does h by Lemma 2.60.

Corollary 2.65 
Every three-dimensional hyperbolicity cone is spectrahedral. 
Proof This follows at once from Corollary 2.64 and Lemma 2.9.

Let us now use the existence of determinantal representations in dimension three 
to prove some already announced geometric properties of general hyperbolicity 
cones. These results were ï¬rst proven in [30], with a more elementary but also more 
technical proof. 
Corollary 2.66 
Every hyperbolicity cone is a convex cone. 
Proof Let h element of double struck upper R left bracket x right bracket Subscript left parenthesis d right parenthesish âˆˆR[x](d) be hyperbolic with respect to e. For  u element of ModifyingAbove script upper C With quotation dash Subscript e Baseline left parenthesis h right parenthesisu âˆˆCe(h) and alpha greater than 0Î± > 0, 
the roots of h left parenthesis alpha u plus t e right parenthesish(Î±u+te) are those of h left parenthesis u plus alpha Superscript negative 1 Baseline t e right parenthesish(u+Î±âˆ’1te), which are just the roots of h left parenthesis u minus t e right parenthesish(uâˆ’te)
multiplied with alphaÎ±. So it is clear that alpha u element of ModifyingAbove script upper C With quotation dash Subscript e Baseline left parenthesis h right parenthesisÎ±u âˆˆCe(h). 
Given two points u comma v element of ModifyingAbove script upper C With quotation dash Subscript e Baseline left parenthesis h right parenthesisu, v âˆˆCe(h), let  V be the three-dimensional subspace spanned 
by e comma u comma ve, u, v. Then ModifyingAbove script upper C With quotation dash Subscript e Baseline left parenthesis h right parenthesis intersection upper VCe(h) âˆ©V is the hyperbolicity cone of h vertical bar Subscript upper V Baseline commah|V , which is spectrahedral 
by Corollary 2.65. In particular it is a convex cone, and therefore contains u plus vu+v.

Corollary 2.67 
If h is hyperbolic with respect to e, then it is hyperbolic with respect to any 
interior point of ModifyingAbove script upper C With quotation dash Subscript e Baseline left parenthesis h right parenthesisCe(h).

2.6
Hyperbolic Polynomials from Graphs 
In this section we will ï¬rst explain an interesting method to produce hyperbolic 
polynomials with deï¬nite determinantal representations, using the Matrix-Tree 
Theorem by Kirchhoff [51], initially presented already in 1847. Is has been used in 
the context of hyperbolic polynomials for example in [15,16]. We will then present 
BrÃ¤ndÃ©nâ€™s result [15] on hyperbolicity cones of elementary symmetric polynomials. 
Let upper G equals left parenthesis upper V comma upper E right parenthesisG = (V, E) be a ï¬nite undirected multigraph without loops. Formally, this 
means that E and V are ï¬nite sets, and a map

into the 2-element subsets of V is also given (specifying the two distinct endpoints 
of each edge). Informally, it means that the ï¬nitely many vertices are connected 
by edges, each edge connecting two different vertices, but there might be (ï¬nitely 
many) multiple edges between any two vertices. The informal approach is sufï¬cient 
here, and we thus suppress phiÏ• from the notation. We will also just say â€œmultigraphâ€ 
instead of â€œï¬nite undirected multigraph without loopsâ€ from now on.

2.7
Derivative Cones 
In this section we collect some results on derivative cones of hyperbolicity cones. 
For a polynomial h which is hyperbolic in direction e, we denote by partial differential Subscript e Baseline hâˆ‚eh its 
derivative in direction e, and call 
ModifyingAbove script upper C With quotation dash Subscript e Baseline left parenthesis partial differential Subscript e Baseline h right parenthesisCe(âˆ‚eh)

notation, since the derivative cone depends on h and not only on the cone ModifyingAbove script upper C With quotation dash Subscript e Baseline left parenthesis h right parenthesisCe(h)).

cones 
ModifyingAbove script upper C With quotation dash Subscript e Baseline left parenthesis partial differential Subscript e Superscript k Baseline h right parenthesis periodCe(âˆ‚k
e h).
In Exercise 2.49 it is shown that the derivative cone always contains the original 
cone, so any such cone admits an increasing sequence of higher order derivative 
cones.

Linear Matrix Inequalities and Spectrahedra
In [54], it has been shown that Theorem 2.82 does hold for the higher derivative 
cones ModifyingAbove script upper C With quotation dash Subscript e Baseline left parenthesis partial differential Subscript e Superscript k Baseline h right parenthesisCe(âˆ‚k

2.8
Free Spectrahedra 
Some interesting facts about spectrahedra and their deï¬ning matrix polynomials can 
only be learned when looking at their free (that means non-commutative) extensions. 
They result by evaluating linear matrix polynomials at tuples of Hermitian matrices, 
instead of just tuples of real numbers. Since matrices do not commute in general 
(i.e., are free of the burden of commutativity), this accounts for the name free 
spectrahedra. In this section we will introduce the most important concepts and 
explain some results. We will restrict to convex cones throughout, but it must be 
pointed out that, while any nonzero number can be rescaled to 1, not every nonzero 
Hermitian matrix can be rescaled to the identity matrix. This makes passing between 
homogeneous and non-homogeneous settings a little more difï¬cult. In this section 
we will also mostly use Hermitian matrices, since the closely related theory of 
operator systems always works in the complex setting. 
Throughout this section, let upper M equals x 0 upper M 0 plus midline horizontal ellipsis plus x Subscript n Baseline upper M Subscript nM = x0M0 + Â· Â· Â· + xnMn be a homogeneous and 
Hermitian linear matrix polynomial of size s, with

for some

â–¶Remark 2.87 The properties of Lemma 2.85 can be summarized by saying that 
script upper S Subscript normal infinity Baseline left parenthesis upper M right parenthesisSâˆ(M) is an abstract operator system (see for example [79, Chapter 13]). But 
it can also be understood as a concrete operator system. Indeed the space 
upper V colon equals s p a n Subscript double struck upper C Baseline left parenthesis upper M 0 comma ellipsis comma upper M Subscript n Baseline right parenthesis subset of or equal to upper M a t Subscript s Baseline left parenthesis double struck upper C right parenthesisV := spanC (M0, . . . , Mn) âŠ†Mats(C)

upper V subset of or equal to upper M a t Subscript s Baseline left parenthesis double struck upper C right parenthesisV âŠ†Mats(C) comes equipped with its set of positive elements of higher level in

levels script upper S Subscript k Baseline left parenthesis upper M right parenthesisSk(M). 
Every abstract operator system can be realized as a concrete operator system on 
a possibly inï¬nite-dimensional space of operators, by the Choi-Effros Theorem 
[79]. But what makes free spectrahedra special (among general abstract operator

a ï¬nite-dimensional space. 
As we will demonstrate in Theorem 2.96 below, they can also be seen as free 
halfspaces, at least in the sense of separation theory.

â–¶Remark 2.91 Checking containment script upper S left parenthesis upper M right parenthesis subset of or equal to script upper S left parenthesis upper N right parenthesisS(M) âŠ†S(N) of classical spectrahedra 
is an NP-hard problem, already in special cases (see for example [35, 50]). In 
case that script upper S left parenthesis upper M right parenthesisS(M) is a cube, a condition similar to Theorem 2.89 (3) has been 
proposed in [6], as a tractable sufï¬cient condition for containment (see also 
[40]). Theorem 2.89 reveals that this approach in fact detects containment of 
free spectrahedra. Since containment at level one does in general not imply 
containment at higher levels (see Example 2.90), it also explains why the 
approach may fail to detect containment of classical spectrahedra. 
There are several cases in which containment of classical spectrahedra does 
imply containment of free spectrahedra (and thus condition (3) from Theorem 2.89), 
see [28,50] for more information:

Note that the ï¬rst step in the previous proof could also be done with a more 
elementary argument, for example as used in the proof of Theorem 3.5 (8) below. 
Using results on free spectrahedra is much less elementary, but avoids a lot of 
technical arguments. 
One would like to completely classify linear matrix polynomials that deï¬ne the 
same spectrahedron. As we have seen already, this is harder than one might suspect, 
since the free spectrahedra impose important conditions that cannot be seen at level 
one alone. However, classifying linear matrix polynomials deï¬ning the same free 
spectrahedron is easier. The following notion and result was also proven in [40].

3
Spectrahedral Shadows 
In this chapter we introduce the notion of a spectrahedral shadow, and examine 
these sets thoroughly. They are still feasible sets for semideï¬nite programming, 
and in contrast to spectrahedra, many of the standard constructions from convexity 
theory can be applied to them. We explain how general semialgebraic sets can be 
approximated by spectrahedral shadows and how these methods provide criteria 
for being a spectrahedral shadow for many sets. While all convex semialgebraic 
sets in the plane are spectrahedral shadows, we also provide examples of convex 
semialgebraic sets that cannot be realized as a projection of a spectrahedron. 
3.1
Spectrahedral Shadows 
A spectrahedral shadow or projected spectrahedron is the image of a spectrahedron 
under an afï¬ne-linear map. Such a shadow is clearly again convex, and also 
semialgebraic (by the Projection Theorem 1.2) but need not be a spectrahedron. 
One obvious reason is that a spectrahedral shadow is not necessarily closed, 
since linear maps are not closed. For example, the projection of the spectrahe-
dron

3
Spectrahedral Shadows
While spectrahedra are very special convex sets, it is still unclear which 
distinguishing features spectrahedral shadows have in general (beyond the obvious 
of being convex and semialgebraic). It was asked in [65] whether every convex 
semialgebraic set is a spectrahedral shadow. This also became known as the Helton-
Nie conjecture [41]. This conjecture was proven by Scheiderer for two-dimensional 
sets [98], but later disproven in higher dimensions [99]. Helton and Nie [41, 42] 
proved a series of results which can be summed up as saying that any convex 
semialgebraic set with sufï¬ciently regular boundary is a spectrahedral shadow. 
Understanding and proving some of these results will be one of our main goals 
in the following sections. 
We will start by establishing some basic properties of spectrahedral shadows. In 
particular, we will show that many of the usual operations preserving convexity also 
preserve the property of being a spectrahedral shadow. 
3.2
Operations on Spectrahedral Shadows 
The following theorem contains many of the constructions that can be performed 
with convex semialgebraic sets, and states that all these operations preserve the 
property of being a spectrahedral shadow. Item (6) was ï¬rst proven in [41], see also 
[64, 71]. Items (8), (9) and thus (10) are already implicitly contained in the duality 
theory of semideï¬nite programming (see for example [87]), and (11) is from [67]. 
Theorem 3.5 
Let upper P comma upper Q subset of or equal to double struck upper R Superscript nP, Q âŠ†Rn, upper R subset of or equal to double struck upper R Superscript mR âŠ†Rm be spectrahedral shadows. Then the following sets

6. Convex hull: c o n v left parenthesis upper P union upper Q right parenthesis periodconv(P âˆªQ).
7. Projective transformations of P.

3.3
Positive Polynomials and the Lasserre-Parrilo Relaxation 
In this section we introduce an important method to systematically and explicitly 
construct spectrahedal shadows, approximating a given semialgebraic set. It is 
described in [57], similar ideas can be found in [76], and we thus call it the Lasserre-
Parrilo relaxation method. In many cases this method can be used to show that the 
convex hull of the initial semialgebraic set is a spectrahedral shadow, as we will 
demonstrate in the following sections. The basic building blocks for Lasserre-Parrilo 
relaxations are truncated quadratic modules and their duals. We brieï¬‚y review the 
deï¬nitions here, and refer to Sect. A in the appendix for more detailed explanations.

â–¶Remark 3.25 (Theta Bodies) A construction closely related to the Lasserre-

optimization problems. It turns out that they are equivalent to Lasserre-Parrilo 
relaxations for real varieties, under some mild assumptions. This is what we will 
now explain.

Corollary 3.35

Finally, we show how to deduce the full two-dimensional Helton-Nie conjecture 
from the above result. This amounts to dealing ï¬rst with closed but not necessarily 
bounded semialgebraic subsets of the plane, and then performing some surgery on 
the boundary in the general case.

3.5
General Exactness Results: The Helton-Nie Theorems 
After having settled the two-dimensional case completely, we now turn to positive 
results in higher dimensions. As we will see in Sect. 3.8, not every convex 
semialgebraic set is a spectrahedral shadow. However, the results of Helton and

92

Nie, which we present in this section, state sufï¬cient conditions for semialgebraic 
sets to be spectrahedral shadows. These conditions can be applied to sets of arbitrary 
dimension, and they even imply the exactness of a Lasserre-Parrilo relaxation. The 
results are all from [41] and [42]. We begin with the easiest case.

A polynomial p whose Hessian is a sum of squares is called sos-convex. By  
Exercise B.24, any such polynomial is convex. But as usual, the converse does not 
hold in general.

Next, we present a more sophisticated version of Theorem 3.38, in which the 
deï¬ning polynomials are not required to be sos-concave. Instead, we will assume

uniform lower bound on the curvature. The proof makes use of Putinarâ€™s Theorem 
for matrix polynomials, as proven in the appendix.

Since not every strictly concave polynomial is sos-concave, it is clear that 
Theorem 3.43 can be applied to examples in which Theorem 3.38 fails. In fact, 
one can even show that most concave polynomials (in a suitable sense) are not sos-
concave (see [8]). But explicit examples of such polynomials (as in [1]) are not easy 
to come by. 
On the other hand, the assumption that the deï¬ning polynomials p Subscript ipi be strictly 
concave on script upper S left parenthesis p right parenthesisS(p) is still quite restrictive. We can further weaken the hypotheses if 
we make use of our freedom in choosing the deï¬ning polynomials p of the basic 
closed set script upper S left parenthesis p right parenthesisS(p). This needs some technical preliminaries.

3.6
Hyperbolicity Cones as Spectrahedral Shadows 
Following [70], we can use the exactness results of Helton and Nie to study 
representations of hyperbolicity cones as spectrahedral shadows. For a hyperbolic 
polynomial h with smooth hyperbolicity cone, this boils down to verifying quasi-
concavity of h on the boundary. But this requires some care, since the concavity 
cannot be strict along lines through the origin, since h is homogeneous. Thus we 
have to take a suitable dehomogenization. First, we will need the following basic 
lemma (for a proof avoiding the use of the Helton-Vinnikov Theorem see [81], 
Lemma 2.4).

3.7
Necessary Conditions for Exactness 
So far we have only seen sufï¬cient conditions for the exactness of the Lasserre-
Parrilo relaxations, but not a single example in which it demonstrably fails to 
become exact. In this chapter, we will ï¬ll this gap. Note however that the results in 
this section do not imply that the corresponding sets are not spectrahedral shadows! 
They just say that the very convenient relaxation method of Lasserre and Parrilo 
fails to prove this property (see Examples 3.62 and 3.65). 
The principal obstruction against exactness is the following result from [31].

Exercise 3.63 
Show that c o n v left parenthesis upper S right parenthesisconv(S) in Example 3.62 is a spectrahedral shadow. 
The necessary condition for exactness in Proposition 3.60 and Corollary 3.61 
depends on the description of the basic closed set S. But there is also a more 
intrinsic geometric condition. We have seen in Theorem 2.26 that spectrahedra 
have only exposed faces. This does not carry over to spectrahedral shadows in 
general, since projections of exposed faces need not be exposed (see Example 3.65 
below). But in fact it carries over to convex sets having an exact Lasserre-Parrilo 
relaxation. This was ï¬rst shown in [68], but can now easily be deduced from 
Proposition 3.60. 
Theorem 3.64 
Let upper S equals script upper S left parenthesis p right parenthesisS = S(p) be convex with non-empty interior. If S has a non-exposed face, 
then no Lasserre-Parrilo relaxation of S with respect to p is exact.

3.8
Generalized Relaxations and Scheidererâ€™s 
Counterexamples 
As we have seen in Sect. 3.7, the existence of an exact Lasserre-Parrilo relaxation 
is only sufï¬cient, but not necessary for a set to be a spectrahedral shadow. 
Only when generalizing the above notion of relaxation, both properties indeed 
become equivalent. This approach can then be used to show that certain convex 
semialgebraic sets are indeed not spectrahedral shadows, answering a question from 
[65], and refuting the Helton-Nie conjecture [41, 42]. This was ï¬rst achieved by 
Scheiderer in [99]. Our presentation follows the similar but slightly more elementary 
approach from [26]. 
The ï¬rst result can be seen as a generalization of Proposition 3.18, using  
semialgebraic functions instead of just polynomials to construct a relaxation. In this 
setting, the existence of bounded degree sums-of-squares representations for linear 
polynomials is really equivalent to being a spectrahedral shadow. For simplicity, we 
restrict to convex cones.

Note that the last statement is clearly also true for symmetric instead of Hermitian 
matrices. Since separable matrices play an important role in quantum physics, and it 
is common to consider Hermitian matrices there, we stated the results for Hermitian 
matrices only.
â–¶Remark 3.77 The given counterexamples show an interesting pattern. A com-
mon theme in polynomial optimization has recently been to transform hard 
optimization problems to easier semideï¬nite problems, by using sums-of-squares 
certiï¬cates instead of positivity (see for example [58] for a comprehensive 
overview). However, this approach is in general not equivalent to the initial 
problem, since sums-of-squares certiï¬cates usually fail to exist for all nonneg-
ative polynomials. The Peres-Horodecki criterion for separability [48, 80] is of  
the same spirit. Corollaries 3.71 and 3.76 (to be precise: the versions with n 
reduced by one, which we did not prove) state that exactly in the cases where 
the approaches become non-equivalent, the corresponding set of nonnegative 
polynomials and separable psd matrices are also not a spectrahedral shadows. 
So it seems like the hard problems resist being made simple.
â–¶Remark 3.78 A completely different approach to the problem in this section, 
using the Tarski principle and convex separation over real closed ï¬elds, has 
recently been developed by Bodirsky et al. in [11]. This has also produced new 
counterexamples, notably the cone of copositive matrices of size 5 times 55 Ã— 5.

A
Real Algebraic Geometry 
This section contains a short introduction to concepts and results from real algebra 
and geometry, with a special focus on (Matrix-)PositivstellensÃ¤tze, stability of 
quadratic modules, and sums of squares on curves. The reader who wants to learn 
more about this exciting topic is referred to the books [10,63,83]. 
A.1 
Semialgebraic Sets, Semialgebraic Mappings 
and Dimension 
Recall that a basic closed semialgebraic set is deï¬ned as 
script upper S left parenthesis p right parenthesis equals script upper S left parenthesis p 1 comma ellipsis comma p Subscript r Baseline right parenthesis equals left brace u element of double struck upper R Superscript n Baseline vertical bar p 1 left parenthesis u right parenthesis greater than or slanted equals 0 comma ellipsis comma p Subscript r Baseline left parenthesis u right parenthesis greater than or slanted equals 0 right brace commaS(p) = S(p1, . . . , pr) =

u âˆˆRn | p1(u) â©¾0, . . . , pr(u) â©¾0

,
for p equals left parenthesis p 1 comma ellipsis comma p Subscript r Baseline right parenthesisp = (p1, . . . , pr) with all p Subscript i Baseline element of double struck upper R left bracket x right bracketpi âˆˆR[x]. A general semialgebraic set is a 
(ï¬nite) Boolean combination of basic closed semialgebraic sets. The Projection 
Theorem 1.2 states that projections of semialgebraic sets are again semialgebraic. 
This innocent-looking result is in fact not completely trivial to prove, and has 
many strong implications, see [10, 63, 83] for more details. For our purpose, 
the most relevant implications are that closure, interior, convex hulls, and many 
more constructions with semialgebraic sets again produce semialgebraic sets (see 
Example 1.4). 
Deï¬nition A.1 
The dimension dimension left parenthesis upper S right parenthesisdim(S) of a semialgebraic set is the dimension of the double struck upper RR-Zariski 
closure of S, as an afï¬ne double struck upper RR-variety. This coincides for example with the maximal 
length of a chain of prime ideals in the ring double struck upper R left bracket x right bracket divided by script upper I left parenthesis upper S right parenthesis commaR[x]/I(S), where script upper I left parenthesis upper S right parenthesisI(S) is the ideal 
of real polynomials vanishing on S.

This result by itself does not help us much: To prove exactness of the Lasserre-
Parrilo relaxation, we would need to show that the degree bound can be chosen 
independently of deltaÎ´ and parallel to q parallel to commaâˆ¥qâˆ¥, at least if q has degree 1. The insight of Helton and Nie 
(as explained in Sect. 3.5) was that this could be proved under suitable regularity 
assumptions with the use of Lagrange functions. Their approach requires a version 
of Putinarâ€™s Theorem for (non-linear) matrix polynomials with degree bounds. We 
will prove this result in Sect. A.3 below.

A.4 
Model-Theoretic Characterization of Stability 
In this section, we will describe some abstract tools that can be used to prove the 
existence of degree bounds for sums of squares and quadratic modules. We will 
assume some familiarity with the theory of real closed ï¬elds, in particular the Tarski 
Transfer Principle, which says that a ï¬rst-order sentence in the language of ordered 
rings holds in one real closed ï¬eld, say double struck upper RR, if and only if it holds in every real closed 
ï¬eld (see for example [10, 63, 83] and also [84]). In fact, we are only interested 
in extension ï¬elds of double struck upper RR and the corresponding extensions of semialgebraic sets: 
If S is a semialgebraic subset of double struck upper R Superscript n Baseline commaRn, and normal upper RR is any real closed extension ï¬eld of 
double struck upper RR, we write upper S left parenthesis normal upper R right parenthesisS(R) for the base extension of S to normal upper RR, which is just the subset of normal upper R Superscript nRn
described by the same formula1 as S. Now an important consequence of the Transfer 
Principle is that upper S left parenthesis normal upper R right parenthesisS(R) is non-empty if and only if S is non-empty. So unlike the 
complex numbers, or the algebraic ï¬eld extensions of double struck upper QQ studied in number theory, 
the purpose of real closed extension ï¬elds of double struck upper RR is not to add solutions to polynomial 
systems of equations and inequalities. The point is rather that solvability remains the 
same, even though the underlying ï¬eld may be radically different from double struck upper RR in other

B
Convexity 
In this section we collect some important notions and results from convexity theory 
and optimization. As general references on convexity, we recommend the books 
[3,90,103], for optimization we refer to [5,13,65]. 
B.1

B.3
Semideï¬nite Programming 
We now give a very brief introduction into the theory of semideï¬nite programming. 
Since spectrahedra and their shadows are precisely the feasible sets for these types 
of optimization problems (as we will see), a short such section must not be missing 
from this book. A semideï¬nite program is a convex optimization problem that can 
be written in the following form, usually called the primal form: 
(P)

B.4
Lagrange Multipliers and Convex Optimization 
The basic idea in Sect. 3.5 is to study representations of (non-linear) Lagrange 
functions of a linear polynomial, in order to represent it as an element of a quadratic 
module. To explain this, we need a bit of background and terminology from general 
(polynomial) optimization. The following is a special case of the Karush-Kuhn-
Tucker theorem.
